         <div>
          Europa's trailing hemisphere in approximate natural color. The prominent crater in the lower right is
          <a href="/wiki/Pwyll_(crater)" title="Pwyll (crater)">
           Pwyll
          </a>
          and the darker regions are areas where Europa's primarily water ice surface has a higher mineral content. Imaged on 7 September 1996 by
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
            Galileo
           </a>
          </i>
          spacecraft.

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_10-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-10">
           [5]
          </a>
         </sup>
         (estimate)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">

          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="nowrap">
          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">

          </span>

         </span>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">

        </td>

         </div>

      <p>
       <b>
        Europa
       </b>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">
            j
           </span>
           <span title="/ʊəˈr/: 'ur' in 'plurality'">
            ʊəˈr
           </span>
           <span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">
            oʊ
           </span>
           <span title="'p' in 'pie'">
            p
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-Europa.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/En-Europa.ogg" title="En-Europa.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         yoor-
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          OH
         </span>
         -pə
        </i>
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USdict_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-USdict-16">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (
       <b>
        Jupiter II
       </b>
       ), is the smallest of the four
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean moons
       </a>
       orbiting
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter#List" title="Moons of Jupiter">
        sixth-closest
       </a>
       to the planet. It is also the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites" title="List of natural satellites">
        sixth-largest moon
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       . Europa was discovered in 1610 by
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUMoonDiscoveries_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUMoonDiscoveries-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and was named after
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(mythology)" title="Europa (mythology)">
        Europa
       </a>
       , the legendary mother of King
       <a href="/wiki/Minos" title="Minos">
        Minos
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">
        Crete
       </a>
       and lover of
       <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">
        Zeus
       </a>
       (the Greek equivalent of the Roman god
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Slightly smaller than
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Earth's Moon
       </a>
       , Europa is primarily made of
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicate
       </a>
       rock and has a water-ice crust
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150315_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150315-17">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and probably an
       <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93nickel_alloy" title="Iron–nickel alloy">
        iron–nickel
       </a>
       core. It has a tenuous atmosphere composed primarily of
       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
        oxygen
       </a>
       . Its surface is striated by cracks and streaks, whereas craters are relatively rare. In addition to Earth-bound telescope observations, Europa has been examined by a succession of space probe flybys, the first occurring in the early 1970s.
      </p>
      <p>
       Europa has the smoothest surface of any known solid object in the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The apparent youth and smoothness of the surface have led to the hypothesis that a
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
        water
       </a>
       ocean exists beneath it, which could conceivably harbor
       <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">
        extraterrestrial life
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tritt2002_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tritt2002-19">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The predominant model suggests that heat from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_flexing" title="Tidal flexing">
        tidal flexing
       </a>
       causes the ocean to remain liquid and drives ice movement similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
        plate tectonics
       </a>
       , absorbing chemicals from the surface into the ocean below.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geology_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-geology-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140908_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140908-21">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sea salt from a subsurface ocean may be coating some geological features on Europa, suggesting that the ocean is interacting with the seafloor. This may be important in determining if Europa could be habitable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150512_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150512-22">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, the
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </a>
       detected
       <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
        water vapor plumes
       </a>
       similar to those observed on Saturn's moon
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       , which are thought to be caused by erupting
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogeyser" title="Cryogeyser">
        cryogeysers
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131212-EU_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131212-EU-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       mission, launched in 1989, provides the bulk of current data on Europa. No spacecraft has yet landed on Europa, although there have been several proposed exploration missions. The
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moon_Explorer" title="Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer">
        Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer
       </a>
       (JUICE) is a mission to
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       that is due to launch in 2022, and will include two flybys of Europa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selection_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-selection-24">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       NASA's planned
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_Clipper" title="Europa Clipper">
        Europa Clipper
       </a>
       will be launched in the mid-2020s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20140304_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AP-20140304-25">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_naming">
        Discovery and naming
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery and naming">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Europa, along with Jupiter's three other large moons,
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">
        Callisto
       </a>
       , was discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       on 8 January 1610,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUMoonDiscoveries_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUMoonDiscoveries-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and possibly independently by
       <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">
        Simon Marius
       </a>
       . The first reported observation of Io and Europa was made by
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       on 7 January 1610 using a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo_Telescope" title="Galileo Telescope">
        20×-magnification refracting telescope
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">
        University of Padua
       </a>
       . However, in that observation, Galileo could not separate Io and Europa due to the low magnification of his telescope, so that the two were recorded as a single point of light. The following day, 8 January 1610 (used as the discovery date for Europa by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        IAU
       </a>
       ), Io and Europa were seen for the first time as separate bodies during Galileo's observations of the Jupiter system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUMoonDiscoveries_1-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUMoonDiscoveries-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Europa is named after
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(mythology)" title="Europa (mythology)">
        Europa
       </a>
       , daughter of the king of
       <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">
        Tyre
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
        Phoenician
       </a>
       noblewoman in
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        Greek mythology
       </a>
       . Like all the Galilean satellites, Europa is named after a lover of
       <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">
        Zeus
       </a>
       , the Greek counterpart of
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       . Europa was courted by Zeus and became the queen of
       <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">
        Crete
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The naming scheme was suggested by
       <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">
        Simon Marius
       </a>
       , who discovered the four satellites independently.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marius1614_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marius1614-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Marius attributed the proposal to
       <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">
        Johannes Kepler
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marius1614_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marius1614-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEDS_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SEDS-28">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The names fell out of favor for a considerable time and were not revived in general use until the mid-20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marazzini_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-marazzini-29">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In much of the earlier
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
        astronomical
       </a>
       literature, Europa is simply referred to by its
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">
        Roman numeral
       </a>
       designation as
       <i>
        <span class="nowrap">
         Jupiter II
        </span>
       </i>
       (a system also introduced by Galileo) or as the "second satellite of Jupiter". In 1892, the discovery of
       <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">
        Amalthea
       </a>
       , whose orbit lay closer to Jupiter than those of the Galilean moons, pushed Europa to the third position. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voyager_probes" title="Voyager probes">
        <i>
         Voyager
        </i>
        probes
       </a>
       discovered three more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_satellites_of_Jupiter" title="Inner satellites of Jupiter">
        inner satellites
       </a>
       in 1979, so Europa is now considered Jupiter's sixth satellite, though it is still sometimes referred to as
       <span class="nowrap">
        Jupiter II
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marazzini_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-marazzini-29">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">
        Orbit and rotation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="245" data-file-width="365" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif/220px-Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif/330px-Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Animation showing Io's
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laplace_resonance" title="Laplace resonance">
          Laplace resonance
         </a>
         with Europa and Ganymede
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Europa orbits Jupiter in just over three and a half days, with an orbital radius of about 670,900 km. With an eccentricity of only 0.009, the orbit itself is nearly circular, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">
        orbital inclination
       </a>
       relative to Jupiter's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equatorial_plane" title="Equatorial plane">
        equatorial plane
       </a>
       is small, at 0.470°.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-datasheet_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-datasheet-30">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Like its fellow
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_satellites" title="Galilean satellites">
        Galilean satellites
       </a>
       , Europa is
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">
        tidally locked
       </a>
       to Jupiter, with one hemisphere of Europa constantly facing Jupiter. Because of this, there is a
       <a href="/wiki/Poles_of_astronomical_bodies" title="Poles of astronomical bodies">
        sub-Jovian point
       </a>
       on Europa's surface, from which Jupiter would appear to hang directly overhead. Europa's
       <a href="/wiki/Prime_meridian" title="Prime meridian">
        prime meridian
       </a>
       is the line intersecting this point.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Research suggests that the tidal locking may not be full, as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-synchronous_rotation" title="Non-synchronous rotation">
        non-synchronous rotation
       </a>
       has been proposed: Europa spins faster than it orbits, or at least did so in the past. This suggests an asymmetry in internal mass distribution and that a layer of subsurface liquid separates the icy crust from the rocky interior.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geissler_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Geissler-12">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The slight eccentricity of Europa's orbit, maintained by the gravitational disturbances from the other Galileans, causes Europa's sub-Jovian point to oscillate around a mean position. As Europa comes slightly nearer to Jupiter, Jupiter's gravitational attraction increases, causing Europa to elongate towards and away from it. As Europa moves slightly away from Jupiter, Jupiter's gravitational force decreases, causing Europa to relax back into a more spherical shape, and creating tides in its ocean. The
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        orbital eccentricity
       </a>
       of Europa is continuously pumped by its
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        mean-motion resonance
       </a>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1997-32">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_flexing" title="Tidal flexing">
        tidal flexing
       </a>
       kneads Europa's interior and gives it a source of heat, possibly allowing its ocean to stay liquid while driving subsurface geological processes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geology_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-geology-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997_32-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1997-32">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ultimate source of this energy is Jupiter's rotation, which is tapped by Io through the tides it raises on Jupiter and is transferred to Europa and Ganymede by the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        orbital resonance
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997_32-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1997-32">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2003_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2003-33">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Scientists analyzing the unique cracks lining Europa found evidence showing that it likely spun around a tilted axis at some point in time. If correct, this would explain many of Europa's features. Europa's immense network of crisscrossing cracks serves as a record of the stresses caused by massive tides in its global ocean. Europa's tilt could influence calculations of how much of its history is recorded in its frozen shell, how much heat is generated by tides in its ocean, and even how long the ocean has been liquid. Its ice layer must stretch to accommodate these changes. When there is too much stress, it cracks. A tilt in Europa's axis could suggest that its cracks may be much more recent than previously thought. The reason is that the direction of the spin pole may change by as much as a few degrees per day, completing one precession period over several months. A tilt also could affect the estimates of the age of Europa's ocean. Tidal forces are thought to generate the heat that keeps Europa's ocean liquid, and a tilt in the spin axis means that more heat is generated by tidal forces. This heat helps the ocean to remain liquid longer. Scientists did not specify when the tilt would have occurred and measurements have not been made of the tilt of Europa's axis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">
        Physical characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europa,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="3000" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Europa%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Europa%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Europa%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Europa%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Europa%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Europa%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europa,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Size comparison of Europa (
         <i>
          lower left
         </i>
         ) with the Moon (
         <i>
          top left
         </i>
         ) and Earth (
         <i>
          right
         </i>
         )
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_quadrangles_on_Europa" title="List of quadrangles on Europa">
        List of quadrangles on Europa
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Europa is slightly smaller than the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       . At just over 3,100 kilometres (1,900 mi) in
       <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">
        diameter
       </a>
       , it is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_moons_by_diameter" title="List of moons by diameter">
        sixth-largest moon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size" title="List of Solar System objects by size">
        fifteenth-largest object
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       . Though by a wide margin the least massive of the Galilean satellites, it is nonetheless more massive than all known moons in the Solar System smaller than itself combined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masses_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Masses-35">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its bulk density suggests that it is similar in composition to the
       <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">
        terrestrial planets
       </a>
       , being primarily composed of
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicate
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
        rock
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">
        Internal structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Internal structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       It is estimated that Europa has an outer layer of
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
        water
       </a>
       around 100 km (62 mi) thick; a part frozen as its crust, and a part as a liquid ocean underneath the ice. Recent
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic-field
       </a>
       data from the
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
        <i>
         Galileo
        </i>
       </a>
       orbiter showed that Europa has an induced magnetic field through interaction with Jupiter's, which suggests the presence of a subsurface conductive layer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2014_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2014-37">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This layer is likely a salty liquid-water ocean. Portions of the crust are estimated to have undergone a rotation of nearly 80°, nearly flipping over (see
       <a href="/wiki/True_polar_wander" title="True polar wander">
        true polar wander
       </a>
       ), which would be unlikely if the ice were solidly attached to the mantle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen2008_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cowen2008-38">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Europa probably contains a
       <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">
        metallic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       core.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kivelson-39">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhatia2017_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2017-40">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">
        Surface features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Surface features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="520" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg/220px-PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg/330px-PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg/440px-PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA01295_Europa_Global_Views_in_Natural_and_Enhanced_Colors.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Approximate natural color (left) and enhanced color (right)
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         view of leading hemisphere
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Europa is the smoothest known object in the Solar System, lacking large-scale features such as mountains and craters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waterworld_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-waterworld-41">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However; according to one theory, Europa's equator may be covered in icy spikes called
       <a href="/wiki/Penitente_(snow_formation)" title="Penitente (snow formation)">
        penitentes
       </a>
       , which may be up to ten meters high, due to direct overhead sunlight on the equator, causing the ice to sublime forming vertical cracks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The prominent markings crisscrossing Europa appear to mainly be
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo_feature" title="Albedo feature">
        albedo features
       </a>
       that emphasize low topography. There are few
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        craters
       </a>
       on Europa, because its surface is tectonically too active and therefore young.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnett1996_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arnett1996-43">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuropaAlbedo_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EuropaAlbedo-44">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Europa's icy crust has an
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       (light reflectivity) of 0.64, one of the highest of all moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-datasheet_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-datasheet-30">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuropaAlbedo_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-EuropaAlbedo-44">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This indicates a young and active surface, based on estimates of the frequency of
       <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
        cometary
       </a>
       bombardment that Europa likely experiences, the surface is about 20 to 180 million years old.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk-45">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is currently no full scientific consensus among the sometimes contradictory explanations for the surface features of Europa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astrobio2007_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Astrobio2007-46">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The radiation level at the surface of Europa is equivalent to a dose of about 5400
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millisievert" title="Millisievert">
        mSv
       </a>
       (540
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%B6ntgen_equivalent_man" title="Röntgen equivalent man">
        rem
       </a>
       ) per day,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ringwald_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ringwald-47">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       an amount of radiation that would cause severe illness or death in human beings exposed for a single day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remeffects_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-remeffects-48">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lineae">
        Lineae
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lineae">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_lineae_on_Europa" title="List of lineae on Europa">
        List of lineae on Europa
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2300" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg/220px-PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg/330px-PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg/440px-PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Realistic-color
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         mosaic of Europa's anti-Jovian hemisphere showing numerous
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lineae" title="Lineae">
          lineae
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_(rotated).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1252" data-file-width="2448" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_%28rotated%29.jpg/220px-PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_%28rotated%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_%28rotated%29.jpg/330px-PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_%28rotated%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_%28rotated%29.jpg/440px-PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_%28rotated%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA20028_-_Europa%27s_varied_surface_features_(rotated).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Enhanced-color view showing the intricate pattern of linear fractures on Europa's surface.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Europa's most striking surface features are a series of dark streaks crisscrossing the entire globe, called
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         <a href="/wiki/Linea" title="Linea">
          lineae
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       (English:
       <span lang="en" xml:lang="en">
        lines
       </span>
       ). Close examination shows that the edges of Europa's crust on either side of the cracks have moved relative to each other. The larger bands are more than 20 km (12 mi) across, often with dark, diffuse outer edges, regular striations, and a central band of lighter material.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geissler1998_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Geissler1998-49">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most likely hypothesis states that the lineae on Europa may have been produced by a series of eruptions of warm ice as the Europan crust spread open to expose warmer layers beneath.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Figueredo2003_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Figueredo2003-50">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The effect would have been similar to that seen in Earth's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceanic_ridge" title="Oceanic ridge">
        oceanic ridges
       </a>
       . These various fractures are thought to have been caused in large part by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_flexing" title="Tidal flexing">
        tidal flexing
       </a>
       exerted by Jupiter. Because Europa is tidally locked to Jupiter, and therefore always maintains the same approximate orientation towards Jupiter, the stress patterns should form a distinctive and predictable pattern. However, only the youngest of Europa's fractures conform to the predicted pattern; other fractures appear to occur at increasingly different orientations the older they are. This could be explained if Europa's surface rotates slightly faster than its interior, an effect that is possible due to the subsurface ocean mechanically decoupling Europa's surface from its rocky mantle and the effects of Jupiter's gravity tugging on Europa's outer ice crust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurford2006_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hurford2006-51">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Comparisons of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
         Voyager
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       spacecraft photos serve to put an upper limit on this hypothetical slippage. The full revolution of the outer rigid shell relative to the interior of Europa occurs over a minimum of 12,000 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kattenhorn1_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kattenhorn1-52">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Studies of
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       images have revealed evidence of
       <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">
        subduction
       </a>
       on Europa's surface, suggesting that, just as the cracks are analogous to ocean ridges,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk1989_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk1989-53">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katternhorn2_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Katternhorn2-54">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       so plates of icy crust analogous to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">
        tectonic plates
       </a>
       on Earth are recycled into the molten interior. Together, the evidence for crustal spreading at bands
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schenk1989_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schenk1989-53">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and convergence at other sites
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katternhorn2_54-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Katternhorn2-54">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       marks the first evidence for
       <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
        plate tectonics
       </a>
       on any world other than Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140908_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140908-21">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
          <span title="This text contradicts material at the article 'Plate tectonics', section 'Icy satellites'. (May 2017)">
           contradictory
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_geological_features">
        Other geological features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Other geological features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Europa" title="List of geological features on Europa">
        List of geological features on Europa
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:374px;max-width:374px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA01092_-_Evidence_of_Internal_Activity_on_Europa.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="2150" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/PIA01092_-_Evidence_of_Internal_Activity_on_Europa.jpg/130px-PIA01092_-_Evidence_of_Internal_Activity_on_Europa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/PIA01092_-_Evidence_of_Internal_Activity_on_Europa.jpg/195px-PIA01092_-_Evidence_of_Internal_Activity_on_Europa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/PIA01092_-_Evidence_of_Internal_Activity_on_Europa.jpg/260px-PIA01092_-_Evidence_of_Internal_Activity_on_Europa.jpg 2x" width="130"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:238px;max-width:238px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europa_chaotic_terrain.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="796" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Europa_chaotic_terrain.jpg/236px-Europa_chaotic_terrain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Europa_chaotic_terrain.jpg/354px-Europa_chaotic_terrain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Europa_chaotic_terrain.jpg/472px-Europa_chaotic_terrain.jpg 2x" width="236"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
         Left: surface features indicative of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_flexing" title="Tidal flexing">
          tidal flexing
         </a>
         : lineae, lenticulae and the
         <a href="/wiki/Conamara_Chaos" title="Conamara Chaos">
          Conamara Chaos
         </a>
         region (close-up, right) where craggy, 250 m high peaks and smooth plates are jumbled together.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Other features present on Europa are circular and elliptical
       <i>
        lenticulae
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       for "freckles"). Many are domes, some are pits and some are smooth, dark spots. Others have a jumbled or rough texture. The dome tops look like pieces of the older plains around them, suggesting that the domes formed when the plains were pushed up from below.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diapir_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-diapir-55">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       One hypothesis states that these lenticulae were formed by
       <a href="/wiki/Diapir" title="Diapir">
        diapirs
       </a>
       of warm ice rising up through the colder ice of the outer crust, much like
       <a href="/wiki/Magma_chamber" title="Magma chamber">
        magma chambers
       </a>
       in Earth's crust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diapir_55-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-diapir-55">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The smooth, dark spots could be formed by
       <a href="/wiki/Meltwater" title="Meltwater">
        meltwater
       </a>
       released when the warm ice breaks through the surface. The rough, jumbled lenticulae (called regions of "chaos"; for example,
       <a href="/wiki/Conamara_Chaos" title="Conamara Chaos">
        Conamara Chaos
       </a>
       ) would then be formed from many small fragments of crust, embedded in hummocky, dark material, appearing like
       <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">
        icebergs
       </a>
       in a frozen sea.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goodman-56">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An alternative hypothesis suggest that lenticulae are actually small areas of chaos and that the claimed pits, spots and domes are artefacts resulting from over-interpretation of early, low-resolution Galileo images. The implication is that the ice is too thin to support the convective diapir model of feature formation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thinice_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-thinice-57">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenberg2008_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greenberg2008-58">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In November 2011, a team of researchers from the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">
        University of Texas at Austin
       </a>
       and elsewhere presented evidence in the journal
       <i>
        Nature
       </i>
       suggesting that many "
       <a href="/wiki/Chaos_terrain" title="Chaos terrain">
        chaos terrain
       </a>
       " features on Europa sit atop vast lakes of liquid water.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europagreatlake_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-europagreatlake-59">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europagreatlakeairhart_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-europagreatlakeairhart-60">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These lakes would be entirely encased in Europa's icy outer shell and distinct from a liquid ocean thought to exist farther down beneath the ice shell. Full confirmation of the lakes' existence will require a space mission designed to probe the ice shell either physically or indirectly, for example, using radar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europagreatlakeairhart_60-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-europagreatlakeairhart-60">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Subsurface_ocean">
        Subsurface ocean
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Subsurface ocean">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EuropaInterior1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1636" data-file-width="1192" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/EuropaInterior1.jpg/220px-EuropaInterior1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/EuropaInterior1.jpg/330px-EuropaInterior1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/EuropaInterior1.jpg/440px-EuropaInterior1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EuropaInterior1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Two possible models of Europa
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Scientists' consensus is that a layer of liquid water exists beneath Europa's surface, and that heat from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_flexing" title="Tidal flexing">
        tidal flexing
       </a>
       allows the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">
        subsurface ocean
       </a>
       to remain liquid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geology_20-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-geology-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenberg_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-greenberg-61">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Europa's surface temperature averages about 110
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        K
       </a>
       (−160
       <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">
        °C
       </a>
       ; −260
       <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">
        °F
       </a>
       ) at the equator and only 50 K (−220 °C; −370 °F) at the poles, keeping Europa's icy crust as hard as granite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyclo_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cyclo-14">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first hints of a subsurface ocean came from theoretical considerations of tidal heating (a consequence of Europa's slightly eccentric orbit and
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        orbital resonance
       </a>
       with the other Galilean moons).
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       imaging team members argue for the existence of a subsurface ocean from analysis of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
         Voyager
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       images.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenberg_61-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-greenberg-61">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most dramatic example is "
       <a href="/wiki/Chaos_terrain" title="Chaos terrain">
        chaos terrain
       </a>
       ", a common feature on Europa's surface that some interpret as a region where the subsurface ocean has melted through the icy crust. This interpretation is controversial. Most geologists who have studied Europa favor what is commonly called the "thick ice" model, in which the ocean has rarely, if ever, directly interacted with the present surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greeley_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-greeley-62">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The best evidence for the thick-ice model is a study of Europa's large craters. The largest impact structures are surrounded by concentric rings and appear to be filled with relatively flat, fresh ice; based on this and on the calculated amount of heat generated by Europan tides, it is estimated that the outer crust of solid ice is approximately 10–30 km (6–19 mi) thick,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including a ductile "warm ice" layer, which could mean that the liquid ocean underneath may be about 100 km (60 mi) deep.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This leads to a volume of Europa's oceans of 3 × 10
       <sup>
        18
       </sup>
       m
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       , between two or three times the volume of Earth's oceans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The thin-ice model suggests that Europa's ice shell may be only a few kilometers thick. However, most planetary scientists conclude that this model considers only those topmost layers of Europa's crust that behave elastically when affected by Jupiter's tides. One example is flexure analysis, in which Europa's crust is modeled as a plane or sphere weighted and flexed by a heavy load. Models such as this suggest the outer elastic portion of the ice crust could be as thin as 200 metres (660 ft). If the ice shell of Europa is really only a few kilometers thick, this "thin ice" model would mean that regular contact of the liquid interior with the surface could occur through open ridges, causing the formation of areas of chaotic terrain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billings_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Billings-67">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">
        Composition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Composition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       orbiter found that Europa has a weak
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">
        magnetic moment
       </a>
       , which is induced by the varying part of the Jovian magnetic field. The field strength at the magnetic equator (about 120
       <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
        nT
       </a>
       ) created by this magnetic moment is about one-sixth the strength of Ganymede's field and six times the value of Callisto's.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimmer_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zimmer-68">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The existence of the induced moment requires a layer of a highly electrically conductive material in Europa's interior. The most plausible candidate for this role is a large subsurface ocean of liquid saltwater.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kivelson-39">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
        <i>
         Voyager
        </i>
        spacecraft
       </a>
       flew past Europa in 1979, scientists have worked to understand the composition of the reddish-brown material that coats fractures and other geologically youthful features on Europa's surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spectrographic evidence suggests that the dark, reddish streaks and features on Europa's surface may be rich in salts such as
       <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">
        magnesium sulfate
       </a>
       , deposited by evaporating water that emerged from within.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord1998_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McCord1998-70">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">
        Sulfuric acid
       </a>
       hydrate is another possible explanation for the contaminant observed spectroscopically.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson2005_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Carlson2005-71">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In either case, because these materials are colorless or white when pure, some other material must also be present to account for the reddish color, and
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
        sulfur
       </a>
       compounds are suspected.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calvin_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Calvin-72">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another hypothesis for the colored regions is that they are composed of abiotic
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">
        organic compounds
       </a>
       collectively called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tholins" title="Tholins">
        tholins
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tholins_Europa_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tholins_Europa-73">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amino_acids_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-amino_acids-74">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL2015_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JPL2015-75">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The morphology of Europa's impact craters and ridges is suggestive of fluidized material welling up from the fractures where
       <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">
        pyrolysis
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Radiolysis" title="Radiolysis">
        radiolysis
       </a>
       take place. In order to generate colored tholins on Europa there must be a source of materials (carbon, nitrogen, and water) and a source of energy to make the reactions occur. Impurities in the water ice crust of Europa are presumed both to emerge from the interior as
       <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">
        cryovolcanic
       </a>
       events that resurface the body, and to accumulate from space as interplanetary dust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tholins_Europa_73-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tholins_Europa-73">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tholins bring important
       <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">
        astrobiological
       </a>
       implications, as they may play a role in prebiotic chemistry and
       <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">
        abiogenesis
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prebiotic_chem_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-prebiotic_chem-76">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coll_2010_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Coll_2010-77">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_heat">
        Sources of heat
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sources of heat">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">
        Tidal heating
       </a>
       occurs through the tidal
       <b>
        friction
       </b>
       and tidal
       <b>
        flexing
       </b>
       processes caused by
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">
        tidal acceleration
       </a>
       : orbital and rotational energy are dissipated as heat in the
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">
        core
       </a>
       of the moon, the internal ocean, and the ice crust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa_FAQ_2012_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Europa_FAQ_2012-79">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Tidal friction
       </dt>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Ocean tides are converted to heat by frictional losses in the oceans and their interaction with the solid bottom and with the top ice crust. In late 2008, it was suggested Jupiter may keep Europa's oceans warm by generating large planetary tidal waves on Europa because of its small but non-zero obliquity. This generates so-called
       <a href="/wiki/Rossby_wave" title="Rossby wave">
        Rossby waves
       </a>
       that travel quite slowly, at just a few kilometers per day, but can generate significant kinetic energy. For the current axial tilt estimate of 0.1 degree, the resonance from Rossby waves would contain 7.3
       <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
        ×
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         18
        </span>
       </sup>
       J of kinetic energy, which is two thousand times larger than that of the flow excited by the dominant tidal forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zyga2008_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zyga2008-80">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler2008_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tyler2008-81">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Dissipation of this energy could be the principal heat source of Europa's ocean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zyga2008_80-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zyga2008-80">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler2008_81-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tyler2008-81">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Tidal flexing
       </dt>
      </dl>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_flexing" title="Tidal flexing">
        Tidal flexing
       </a>
       kneads Europa's interior and ice shell, which becomes a source of heat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Depending on the amount of tilt, the heat generated by the ocean flow could be 100 to thousands of times greater than the heat generated by the flexing of Europa's rocky core in response to gravitational pull from Jupiter and the other moons circling that planet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Europa's seafloor could be heated by the moon's constant flexing, driving hydrothermal activity similar to undersea volcanoes in Earth's oceans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa_FAQ_2012_79-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Europa_FAQ_2012-79">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Experiments and ice modeling published in 2016, indicate that tidal flexing dissipation can generate one order of magnitude more heat in Europa's ice than scientists had previously assumed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stacey_2016_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stacey_2016-84">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarthy_2016_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McCarthy_2016-85">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their results indicates that most of the heat generated by the ice actually comes from the ice's
       <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">
        crystalline structure
       </a>
       (lattice) as a result of deformation, and not friction between the ice grains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stacey_2016_84-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stacey_2016-84">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarthy_2016_85-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McCarthy_2016-85">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The greater the deformation of the ice sheet, the more heat is generated.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Radioactive decay
       </dt>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In addition to tidal heating, the interior of Europa could also be heated by the decay of radioactive material (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic_heating" title="Radiogenic heating">
        radiogenic heating
       </a>
       ) within the rocky mantle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa_FAQ_2012_79-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Europa_FAQ_2012-79">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       But the models and values observed are one hundred times higher than those that could be produced by radiogenic heating alone,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       thus implying that tidal heating has a leading role in Europa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plumes">
        Plumes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Plumes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_(artist%27s_impression).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_%28artist%27s_impression%29.jpg/220px-Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_%28artist%27s_impression%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_%28artist%27s_impression%29.jpg/330px-Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_%28artist%27s_impression%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_%28artist%27s_impression%29.jpg/440px-Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_%28artist%27s_impression%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_vapour_plumes_on_Jupiter%27s_moon_Europa_(artist%27s_impression).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Water vapor plumes on Jupiter's moon Europa (artist's impression)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plumes_2013_89-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Plumes_2013-89">
           [83]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </a>
       acquired an image of Europa in 2012 that was interpreted to be a plume of water vapour erupting from near its south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plumes_2013_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plumes_2013-89">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The image suggests the plume may be 200 km (120 mi) high, or more than 20 times the height of Mt. Everest.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131212-EU_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131212-EU-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has been suggested that if they exist, they are episodic
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and likely to appear when Europa is at its farthest point from Jupiter, in agreement with
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">
        tidal force
       </a>
       modeling predictions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa_tidal_forces_2013_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Europa_tidal_forces_2013-93">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additional imaging evidence from the Hubble Space Telescope was presented in September 2016.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plumes_2016_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-plumes_2016-94">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1280" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg/220px-Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg/330px-Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg/440px-Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Photo_composite_of_suspected_water_plumes_on_Europa.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Photo composite of suspected water plumes on Europa.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
          <a href="#cite_note-96">
           [90]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The tidal forces are about 1,000 times stronger than the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       's effect on
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       . The only other moon in the Solar System exhibiting water vapor plumes is
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131212-EU_23-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131212-EU-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The estimated eruption rate at Europa is about 7000 kg/s
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa_tidal_forces_2013_93-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Europa_tidal_forces_2013-93">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       compared to about 200 kg/s for the plumes of Enceladus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen2006_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hansen2006-97">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer2013_98-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer2013-98">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If confirmed, it would open the possibility of a flyby through the plume and obtain a sample to analyze
       <i>
        in situ
       </i>
       without having to use a lander and drill through miles of ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plumes_2016_94-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-plumes_2016-94">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europa_plume_in_situ_100-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-europa_plume_in_situ-100">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">
        Atmosphere
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Europa_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Observations with the
       <a href="/wiki/Goddard_High_Resolution_Spectrograph" title="Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph">
        Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </a>
       , first described in 1995, revealed that Europa has a thin
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body%27s_atmosphere" title="Celestial body's atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       composed mostly of
       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
        molecular oxygen
       </a>
       (O
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall1995_101-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hall1995-101">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuropaOxygenJPL_102-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EuropaOxygenJPL-102">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The surface pressure of Europa's atmosphere is 0.1
       <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">
        μPa
       </a>
       , or 10
       <sup>
        −12
       </sup>
       times that of the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGrathChapter_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McGrathChapter-15">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1997, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft confirmed the presence of a tenuous
       <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">
        ionosphere
       </a>
       (an upper-atmospheric layer of charged particles) around Europa created by solar radiation and energetic particles from Jupiter's
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetosphere
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kliore1997_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kliore1997-103">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1997_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA1997-104">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       providing evidence of an atmosphere.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europa_field.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1155" data-file-width="1023" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Europa_field.png/170px-Europa_field.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Europa_field.png/255px-Europa_field.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Europa_field.png/340px-Europa_field.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europa_field.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Magnetic field around Europa. The red line shows a trajectory of the
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         spacecraft during a typical flyby (E4 or E14).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Unlike the oxygen in
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">
        Earth's atmosphere
       </a>
       , Europa's is not of biological origin. The surface-bounded atmosphere forms through
       <a href="/wiki/Radiolysis" title="Radiolysis">
        radiolysis
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">
        dissociation
       </a>
       of molecules through radiation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson1982_105-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Johnson1982-105">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Solar ultraviolet radiation and charged particles (ions and electrons) from the Jovian magnetospheric environment collide with Europa's icy surface, splitting water into oxygen and hydrogen constituents. These chemical components are then
       <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">
        adsorbed
       </a>
       and "
       <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">
        sputtered
       </a>
       " into the atmosphere. The same radiation also creates collisional ejections of these products from the surface, and the balance of these two processes forms an atmosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shematovich2003_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shematovich2003-106">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Molecular oxygen is the densest component of the atmosphere because it has a long lifetime; after returning to the surface, it does not stick (freeze) like a water or
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">
        hydrogen peroxide
       </a>
       molecule but rather desorbs from the surface and starts another
       <a href="/wiki/External_ballistics" title="External ballistics">
        ballistic
       </a>
       arc. Molecular hydrogen never reaches the surface, as it is light enough to escape Europa's surface gravity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liang_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Liang-107">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smyth_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smyth-108">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Observations of the surface have revealed that some of the molecular oxygen produced by radiolysis is not ejected from the surface. Because the surface may interact with the subsurface ocean (considering the geological discussion above), this molecular oxygen may make its way to the ocean, where it could aid in biological processes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chyba_109-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chyba-109">
